<div id="review_333862383" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/333862383" />
    <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em"><img alt="Jennifer (aka EM)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510513814p2/1393336.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/333862383?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer (aka EM)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer (aka EM)" href="/user/show/1393336-jennifer-aka-em">Jennifer (aka EM)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156028776" href="/book/show/227581.The_Crimson_Petal_and_the_White">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1393336-jennifer-aka-em?shelf=lonely-hearts-club">lonely-hearts-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer333862383" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11598394756674611210">Fantastic. The perfect book to end a great year of reads. <br/>---------<br/><br/>Come for the sexy story, stay for the character portraits and technique. There are lots of good reviews of this (see <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13506833?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Kelly's</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6877010?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Paul's</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249591596?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cecily's</a>, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206999045?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Trevor's</a>, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423278012?book_show_action=false&page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'>Simon's</a>) - lots of them focus on the length (ho, ho, ho), so besides the rampant double entendres that popped up everywhere, I’ll just tell you a dozen more things that I *loved* about TCP:<br/><br/>1. The narrator: without him/it, the ending would have been unthinkable. And the endin</span>
  <span id="freeText11598394756674611210" style="display:none">Fantastic. The perfect book to end a great year of reads. <br />---------<br /><br />Come for the sexy story, stay for the character portraits and technique. There are lots of good reviews of this (see <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/13506833?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Kelly's</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6877010?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Paul's</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249591596?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Cecily's</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/206999045?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Trevor's</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423278012?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Simon's</a>) - lots of them focus on the length (ho, ho, ho), so besides the rampant double entendres that popped up everywhere, I’ll just tell you a dozen more things that I *loved* about TCP:<br /><br />1. The narrator: without him/it, the ending would have been unthinkable. And the ending is perfect. No, I’m not going to read <i>The Apple</i>. The way the narrator treated the reader – the subtlety of its chastisement, the ironic/sardonic tone, the allusions to the future – very meta. Early on, the narrator teased the reader about just wanting to get to the sexy bits. The way Faber turned that into something so much deeper, and then – the ending; “you imagine you can make it last forever”; the exhortation to “let me go.” More double entendres. Me = a book, on the surface of it. Yet, what else? That’s the real question at the end of this; not the fate of the characters, imho. <br /><br />2. Also a structural thing: The ‘following’ theme – related to the social-climbing theme/structure. The narrator follows Caroline to Sugar to Agnes to William and back to Sugar – then it leaves us with Sugar – who starts following Agnes and William (literally – plot-wise) and then Sophie. This occurs in sections that take us from the streets (of St. Giles) to Mrs. Castaway’s to … well, read it. Another play on convention here, I think: isn’t there a name for novels that travel a specific geography? Whatever, I found these structures to be SUPER-clever.<br /><br />3. The feminist perspective.<br /><br />4. Victorian sexuality and repression seen through the eyes of all its major archetypes:<br />a. The wife driven mad by boredom, repressed sexual desire and enforced ignorance, Agnes<br />b. The tart with a heart, Sugar<br />c. The self-made manufacturing baron, William<br />d. The profligate whoremongers and cads, Bodley &amp; Ashwell<br />e. The social reformer / early suffragette, Emmeline Fox<br />f. The doctor with the bag of tricks, Dr. Curlew<br />g. The puritanical self-abuser, Henry <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[whose demise was mythical; inflamed=enflamed. A play on Dickens’ spontaneous combustion in <i>Bleak House</i>? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />h. The next wave, “I want to be an explorer”, the-future-will-be-different Sophie<br /><br />5. Sugar’s anger. Sugar’s immense, post-traumatic, repressed-memory driven anger.<br /><br />6. The inner emotion/motivation and the outer expression of it, modulated, often self-repressed and directed depending on each character’s nature (or nurture?). Speaking of which…<br /><br />7. Mothering, lack of it. Parenting in general. Sophie’s aloneness, her isolation. SUGAR’s aloneness and isolation … SUGAR’S SELF-MOTHERING. The relationship between Sugar &amp; Sophie.<br /><br />8. Agnes’s boredom and ‘illness’. The fact we know <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[her crazy was because of a brain tumour  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> it was entirely a product of the times. This is what repression – every form, intellectual, sexual, social – does to people.<br /><br />9. The names: Sugar. Mrs. Fox. Agnes (from the Greek, ‘pure’, ‘chaste’, ‘holy’). William and Henry (the later allusion to Sugar creating a rhyme for Sophie to remember all the kings of England because there are so many “Williams and Henrys”). Sophia: "wisdom" in Greek; name of saint who died of grief after her three daughters were martyred. Sugar, Agnes … but not Sophie herself. <br /><br />10. The sensory detail. Sugar’s psoriasis; the focus on scent: perfumes/lavender in contrast to the faeces and piss-ridden stench of St. Giles Street. The fact that when Sugar goes back, she can’t stand the smell. The entire use of perfumery/lavender – as a business concern, as an artificial tie-in to the natural world, as something that covers up the smell of poverty.<br /><br />11. The importance of each of them writing their story: Sugar with her novel (“it’s not going to be one of those ‘Reader, I married him’ types” – see point above and literary allusions; there are plenty to the Brontës – “those Bell sisters; what was their name again?” (I’m paraphrasing Sugar here)). Failed / pretentious, to-be-novelist, William. Agnes’s not-a-diary diary / Book. What happened at the end to these books, and how that ties in with the end's exhortation to "let me go."<br /><br />12. The literary allusions. The book is called the ‘modern Dickens’ – but then that’s what we call all these new pseudo-Victorians, no? I don’t think that’s exactly what Faber was doing here – he put Dickens right into the story and it felt to me more like he was saying, hey – you reader of Dickens, wipe the romanticism from your eyes when it comes to this time, this place: it’s far darker, far worse, far MORE REAL than you ever imagined. Another good one in this vein is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/824986.The_Quincunx" title="The Quincunx by Charles Palliser" rel="nofollow noopener">The Quincunx</a>, which – if you’ve read TCP and liked it, is highly recommended.<br /><br />One of my top reads of 2014. Not to be missed, if you like this kind of thing. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11598394756674611210" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3792559863226791416"><b>3.5 stars - It was really good.</b><br/><br/>The way Faber wrote this novel reminded me of a Dickens’ tale – atmospheric with memorable, flawed characters and an underlying examination of the societal class divides. Some parts were more interesting than others and at 900 pages it felt a bit long in the tooth (also reminiscent of Dickens). I personally preferred his other novel, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20697435.The_Book_of_Strange_New_Things' title='The Book of Strange New Things by Michel Faber' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Book of Strange New Things</a>, to this one, though both were satisfying worthy reads. <br/><br/>-------------------------------------------<br/><b>Fav</b></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_345077220" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345077220" />
    <a title="Olivier Delaye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9854601-olivier-delaye"><img alt="Olivier Delaye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1582470792p2/9854601.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/345077220?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivier Delaye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivier Delaye" href="/user/show/9854601-olivier-delaye">Olivier Delaye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345077220" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18257004551585549420">A wonderful trip back in time to Victorian London is what The Crimson Petal and the White is to me, a slice of life about a young prostitute named Sugar whose determination to pull herself out of the gutter and rise in society embodies in itself the very definition of rags-to-riches. I was hooked from the beginning, and while this book is positively huge, it also possesses that page-turning quality which will surely keep you awake many a sleepless night. I only found the ending somewhat wanting,</span>
  <span id="freeText18257004551585549420" style="display:none">A wonderful trip back in time to Victorian London is what The Crimson Petal and the White is to me, a slice of life about a young prostitute named Sugar whose determination to pull herself out of the gutter and rise in society embodies in itself the very definition of rags-to-riches. I was hooked from the beginning, and while this book is positively huge, it also possesses that page-turning quality which will surely keep you awake many a sleepless night. I only found the ending somewhat wanting, hence my giving it four stars instead of five.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18257004551585549420" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_26771705" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/26771705" />
    <a title="Beth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1310564-beth"><img alt="Beth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589502601p2/1310564.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/26771705?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth" href="/user/show/1310564-beth">Beth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156028776" href="/book/show/227581.The_Crimson_Petal_and_the_White">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1310564-beth?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1310564-beth?shelf=best-book-i-read-that-year">best-book-i-read-that-year</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1310564-beth?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1310564-beth?shelf=victorian">victorian</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26771705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4003434425604746588"><i>Watch your step. Keep your wits about you; you will need them. This city I am bringing you to is vast and intricate, and you have not been here before. You may imagine, from other stories you've read, that you know it well, but those stories flattered you, welcoming you as a friend, treating you as if you belonged. The truth is that you are an alien from another time and place altogether...</i><br/><br/>What a beginning! I passed my copy on after I originally read this in 2005. I gave it to my mom who gave it</span>
  <span id="freeText4003434425604746588" style="display:none"><i>Watch your step. Keep your wits about you; you will need them. This city I am bringing you to is vast and intricate, and you have not been here before. You may imagine, from other stories you've read, that you know it well, but those stories flattered you, welcoming you as a friend, treating you as if you belonged. The truth is that you are an alien from another time and place altogether...</i><br /><br />What a beginning! I passed my copy on after I originally read this in 2005. I gave it to my mom who gave it to her sister who gave it to her daughter. Mom and aunt <i>loved</i> it and the three of us still go into raptures over this book whenever we're all in a room together. My cousin wasn't a big fan; she thought the descriptions were too much, but she’s a much sweeter girl than me, my mom and my aunt. <br /><br />Faber's descriptions of Victorian England are both vulgar and gorgeous. He doesn't mince words. This book is definitely not for the faint at heart. The main character is a prostitute and while Faber doesn’t go into detail about the ACT of sex, there are a number of graphic descriptions about the steps women take to prevent pregnancy, terminate pregnancy, treat illness, etc. <br /><br />There is also so much more to this book. It also explores the struggle of women living in a man’s world and it almost becomes a psychological study. The end is rather abrupt, but Sugar does something unthinkable and morally wrong, and yet I <i>loved</i> the end because I realized that if you’ve been pushed past your breaking point and there is literally no other option out there, something the only thing left to do is the “wrong thing” unless you want to give up and succumb entirely to the wishes and demands of others. Given the circumstances, I suspect that if I were in the same position, I probably would have done the same thing Sugar did, as horrible as it was. <br /><br />Anyway, a few weeks ago I found a copy on the discount shelf at Half Price Books. $2, yes! This was a wonderful book...one of my all-time favorites and I can't wait to read it again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4003434425604746588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_708773079" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Bloodorange" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange"><img alt="Bloodorange" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386872100p2/23066833.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/708773079?book_show_action=true">Sep 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bloodorange" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bloodorange" href="/user/show/23066833-bloodorange">Bloodorange</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=victoriana">victoriana</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=netherlands">netherlands</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23066833-bloodorange?shelf=random-or-not-recs-for-students">random-or-not-recs-for-students</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer708773079" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5483898930483036902">I read this books a few months before joining GR, and shelved it as one of my first books, giving it five stars. I remember it was after I went back to work after the maternity leave, and it was still dark and wintery outside, and my child must have been around eight months, and I remember reading this over a weekend, Friday to Sunday night, lying across our double bed and only leaving the bedroom and the book when I absolutely had to. <br/><br/>I loved this book. It sucked me in, fed me details I sometim</span>
  <span id="freeText5483898930483036902" style="display:none">I read this books a few months before joining GR, and shelved it as one of my first books, giving it five stars. I remember it was after I went back to work after the maternity leave, and it was still dark and wintery outside, and my child must have been around eight months, and I remember reading this over a weekend, Friday to Sunday night, lying across our double bed and only leaving the bedroom and the book when I absolutely had to. <br /><br />I loved this book. It sucked me in, fed me details I sometimes didn't want to know, handed me the Victorian world and the underworld and the inner world on a silver platter. Although it is risqué, I recommend it to my students as a way of helping them understand some of the details of the Victorian mentality. It obviously is a little exaggerated, but in just the way to make this period interesting to people who are put off by the thought of covered table legs. Say, a David Fincher adaptation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5483898930483036902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('OeDdZjs0SxCEt+0QeWhkP/c81kEU0uuTO3Y4h0hJXdVBt0W3uKICGWl5LMMg+yCvIGca581vwP8YQA3oQCnLhg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('d66qybyL4L77qRVCX+TPOkDyhl5S0JOqHTCAupL73TIP+TIYPx2ptxZn1JEGd4uql6lK+IttuMY+BrXVmptLYQ==')})
  }

The suspension of disbelief happens easily, as the reader is led through twisted family trees and plot lines. \n\nThe quincunx of the title is a heraldic figure of five parts that appears at crucial points within the text (the number five recurs throughout the novel, which itself is divided into five parts, one for each of the family galaxies whose orbits the narrator is pulled into).           }
        });
      });

//]]>
</script>

</li>
</ul>
</div>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/16272.Michel_Faber">About Michel Faber</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17776453409895661636">Michel Faber (born 13 April 1960) is a Dutch writer of English-language fiction.<br/><br/>Faber was born in The Hague, The Netherlands. He and his parents emigrated to Australia in 1967. He attended primary and secondary school in the Melbourne suburbs of Boronia and Bayswater, then attended the University Of Melbourne, studying Dutch, Philosophy, Rhetoric, English Language (a course involving translation a</span>
  <span id="freeText17776453409895661636" style="display:none">Michel Faber (born 13 April 1960) is a Dutch writer of English-language fiction.<br /><br />Faber was born in The Hague, The Netherlands. He and his parents emigrated to Australia in 1967. He attended primary and secondary school in the Melbourne suburbs of Boronia and Bayswater, then attended the University Of Melbourne, studying Dutch, Philosophy, Rhetoric, English Language (a course involving translation and criticism of Anglo-Saxon and Middle English texts) and English Literature. He graduated in 1980. He worked as a cleaner and at various other casual jobs, before training as a nurse at Marrickville and Western Suburbs hospitals in Sydney. He nursed until the mid-1990s. In 1993 he, his second wife and family emigrated to Scotland, where they still reside.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17776453409895661636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
